reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes):
        """
        LeNet-5 architecture implementation in PyTorch.

        :param num_classes: The number of output classes.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        # Convolutional layers
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels=1, out_channels=6, kernel_size=5, stride=1)
        self.conv2 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels=6, out_channels=16, kernel_size=5, stride=1)
        
        # Fully connected layers
        self.fc1 = nn.Linear(in_features=16*5*5, out_features=120)
        self.fc2 = nn.Linear(in_features=120, out_features=84)
        self.fc3 = nn.Linear(in_features=84, out_features=num_classes)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass of the LeNet-5 model.

        :param x: The input tensor, shape (batch_size, 1, 32, 32)
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        # First convolutional layer with ReLU activation and max pooling
        x = F.relu(self.conv1(x))
        x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        
        # Second convolutional layer with ReLU activation and max pooling
        x = F.relu(self.conv2(x))
        x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        
        # Flatten the output for the fully connected layers
        x = x.view(-1, 16*5*5)
        
        # First fully connected layer with ReLU activation
        x = F.relu(self.fc1(x))
        
        # Second fully connected layer with ReLU activation
        x = F.relu(self.fc2(x))
        
        # Final fully connected layer
        x = self.fc3(x)
        
        return x

# Test code for the LeNet-5 model (larger batch & image)
batch_size = 4096
num_classes = 20

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 1, 32, 32)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

Conv2d: 1->6, k=5, s=1 -> ReLU -> MaxPool2d(2,2) Input: (B, 1, 32, 32) After conv1: (B, 6, 28, 28) After pool: (B, 6, 14, 14) 2. Conv2d: 6->16, k=5, s=1 -> ReLU -> MaxPool2d(2,2) After conv2: (B, 16, 10, 10) After pool: (B, 16, 5, 5) 3. Flatten to (B, 400) 4. FC1: 400->120 -> ReLU 5. FC2: 120->84 -> ReLU 6. FC3: 84->num_classes The main bottleneck for small batch might be the overhead of multiple kernel launches. We want to fuse operations where possible. For a batch size of 4096, the total compute is: - Conv1: B * 6 * 28 * 28 * 1 * 5 * 5 = 4096 * 6 * 784 * 25 ≈ 482M MACs - Conv2: B * 16 * 10 * 10 * 6 * 5 * 5 = 4096 * 16 * 100 * 150 ≈ 983M MACs - FC1: B * 400 * 120 = 4096 * 48000 ≈ 197M MACs - FC2: B * 120 * 84 = 4096 * 10080 ≈ 41M MACs - FC3: B * 84 * num_classes = 4096 * 84 * 20 ≈ 6.9M MACs Total ~1.7 GFLOPs, which is small. Memory bandwidth might not be the bottleneck, but kernel launch overhead could be. For H100 (67 TFLOPS), 1.7 GFLOPs should take ~25 microseconds at peak, but with many kernel launches it will be much slower. We should fuse operations aggressively. One approach is to use cuDNN via torch, but that's the reference. Another is to write custom CUDA kernels that fuse multiple operations. No. Actually, cublas doesn't know about row vs column major. It just reads memory. The standard trick is: To compute C = A * B where A is M x K and B is K x N, both row-major: cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, alpha, B, N, A, K, beta, C, N); Wait, the cublas convention is: - op(A) is m x k - op(B) is k x n - C is m x n And the leading dimensions are based on the original (untransposed) storage. If A is M x K row-major (lda = K), and we want op(A) = A (M x K): In cublas, if we say transA = N, then cublas reads A as column-major, which would be K x M. But our data is M x K row-major. In column-major, an M x K row-major array is equivalent to a K x M column-major array. So if we have a row-major M x K matrix, from cublas column-major perspective it looks like a K x M matrix with leading dimension K. To get an M x K matrix in the cublas operation, we should pass transA=T and the matrix dimensions K x M. Actually, the standard mapping for row-major GEMM is: C[M,N] = A[M,K] * B[K,N] In cublas: C = B^T * A^T, but with appropriate dimensions... Wait, cublasSgemm computes C = alpha * op(A) * op(B) + beta * C, where op(A) is m x k and op(B) is k x n. If we call with transB=N, transA=N, ldb=N, lda=K, and dimensions: m=M, n=N, k=K. Then cublas expects: - op(A) is M x K, stored column-major with lda. But our A is row-major M x K. In column-major, this is a K x M matrix. So cublas will read it as K x M, not M x K. - To read our row-major M x K as M x K in the operation, we can use transA=T. Then cublas reads the transpose of our storage. Our storage as column-major is K x M. The transpose is M x K. Perfect! Similarly for B: our B is row-major K x N. As column-major, it's N x K. To use it as K x N, we need transB=T. Then cublas reads the transpose, which is K x N. So: C = A^T * B^T, but A and B are our row-major arrays passed with trans=T. Actually, there's a fundamental property: a row-major M x N array stored in memory is exactly the same as a column-major N x M array. The leading dimension in column-major is N (the number of rows in the row-major interpretation). So PyTorch weight (120, 400) row-major = (400, 120) column-major. For non-transposed A (M x K) and transposed B (N x K), computing C = A @ B^T, the call is effectively: cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_T, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, alpha, B, K, A, K, beta, C, N); Wait, let me not guess. I'll use the simplest approach: use cublas with the correct transposes by working through the math. Our data is all row-major. We want C[M,N] = A[M,K] * B[K,N]. This is equivalent to C^T[N,M] = B^T[N,K] * A^T[K,M] in column-major. In cublas column-major, to compute C^T = B^T * A^T: - A_mat = B^T, shape N x K column-major. Our B is K x N row-major, which is N x K column-major. Good. transa = N, lda = N. - B_mat = A^T, shape K x M column-major. Our A is M x K row-major, which is K x M column-major. Let me design the kernels: **Kernel 1: conv1_relu_maxpool** - Grid: (num_blocks), Block: (32) one warp per block. - num_blocks = batch_size * 6 = 24,576 - Each block handles one (batch, c). - threadIdx.x = 0..31, but only 0..24 are active (25 threads for 5x5=25 pooled pixels). - Mask: if (threadIdx.x >= 25) return; ``` __global__ void conv1_relu_maxpool_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size ) { int bc = blockIdx.x; int batch = bc / 6; int c = bc % 6; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid >= 25) return; int ph = tid / 5; int pw = tid % 5; float b = bias[c]; float max_val = 0.0f; // since relu, max with 0 for (int ph_off = 0; ph_off < 2; ++ph_off) { for (int pw_off = 0; pw_off < 2; ++pw_off) { int h = ph * 2 + ph_off; int w = pw * 2 + pw_off; float sum = b; // unroll 5x5 loop #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 5; ++kw) { float inp = input[batch * 1024 + (h + kh) * 32 + (w + kw)]; float wgt = weight[c * 25 + kh * 5 + kw]; sum += inp * wgt; } } float val = sum > 0 ? sum : 0; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } output[batch * 1176 + c * 196 + ph * 14 + pw] = max_val; } ``` Wait, for conv1, input channels = 1. So input indexing is [batch, 0, h+kh, w+kw]. I'll use batch * 1024 + (h+kh)*32 + (w+kw). Actually, I should parameterize the dimensions properly. For the weight indexing: weight[c * 25 + kh * 5 + kw]. That's for 1 input channel. Let me think about the output shape. After pool, it's (B, 6, 14, 14) = B * 1176 elements. Wait, 6*14*14 = 1176. Yes. **Kernel 2: conv2_relu_maxpool** - Grid: batch_size * 16 = 65,536 blocks - Block: 32 threads - Active: 25 threads - Each pooled pixel needs 4 conv values. ``` __global__ void conv2_relu_maxpool_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, // (B, 6, 14, 14) const float* __restrict__ weight, // (16, 6, 5, 5) const float* __restrict__ bias, // (16,) float* __restrict__ output, // (B, 16, 5, 5) int batch_size ) { int bc = blockIdx.x; int batch = bc / 16; int c = bc % 16; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid >= 25) return; int ph = tid / 5; int pw = tid % 5; float b = bias[c]; float max_val = 0.0f; for (int ph_off = 0; ph_off < 2; ++ph_off) { for (int pw_off = 0; pw_off < 2; ++pw_off) { int h = ph * 2 + ph_off; int w = pw * 2 + pw_off; float sum = b; for (int ic = 0; ic < 6; ++ic) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 5; ++kw) { float inp = input[batch * 1176 + ic * 196 + (h + kh) * 14 + (w + kw)]; float wgt = weight[((c * 6 + ic) * 5 + kh) * 5 + kw]; sum += inp * wgt; } } } float val = sum > 0 ? sum : 0; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } output[batch * 400 + c * 25 + ph * 5 + pw] = max_val; } ``` Note: for conv2, input is (B, 6, 14, 14). offset = batch * 1176 + ic * 196 + (h+kh)*14 + (w+kw). **Kernel 3: fused_fc** As discussed, 256 threads per block, 16 samples per block. Total blocks = 256. Here's a more detailed design: Shared memory per block: - float s_input[16][400] // 25.6KB - float s_fc1[16][120] // 7.7KB - float s_fc2[16][84] // 5.4KB Total: 38.7KB Actually, we don't need to store s_fc2 in shared memory if we compute FC3 directly from s_fc2 while it's in registers. But s_fc2 needs to be accessible to all threads in the sample. So yes, shared memory. For each sample in the block, 16 threads cooperate. ``` __global__ void fused_fc_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, // (B, 400) const float* __restrict__ w1, // (120, 400) const float* __restrict__ b1, // (120,) const float* __restrict__ w2, // (84, 120) const float* __restrict__ b2, // (84,) const float* __restrict__ w3, // (20, 84) const float* __restrict__ b3, // (20,) float* __restrict__ output, // (B, num_classes) int batch_size, int num_classes ) { int block_sample_start = blockIdx.x * 16; int tid = threadIdx.x; int sample = tid / 16; int local_tid = tid % 16; // No need for shared memory for input if we stream it, but let's use shared __shared__ float s_input[16][400]; __shared__ float s_fc1[16][120]; __shared__ float s_fc2[16][84]; int s = block_sample_start + sample; // Load input for this sample if (s < batch_size) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 25; ++i) { int idx = local_tid + i * 16; s_input[sample][idx] = input[s * 400 + idx]; } } __syncthreads(); // FC1 for (int o = 0; o < 120; ++o) { float partial = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 25; ++i) { int idx = local_tid + i * 16; partial += s_input[sample][idx] * w1[o * 400 + idx]; } // Reduce across 16 threads // Use warp shuffle. Since 16 threads are in the same warp half, // we can use __shfl_down_sync with mask 0xFFFF. // But we have two samples per warp. Need to use correct mask. int lane = tid % 32; int warp_mask; if (lane < 16) { warp_mask = 0x0000FFFF << (lane < 16 ? 0 : 16); // wait, this is wrong } // Actually, for sample 0: threads 0..15 are in warp 0, lane 0..15. // For sample 1: threads 16..31 are in warp 0, lane 16..31. // So all 16 threads for one sample are contiguous within a warp. // We can use a mask that covers lanes 0..15 or 16..31. int base_lane = (tid / 16) * 16; // 0 or 16, 32 or 48, etc. int my_lane = tid % 32; unsigned int mask = 0xFFFF << base_lane; // base_lane is 0 or 16 within a warp // reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(mask, partial, offset); } if (local_tid == 0) { s_fc1[sample][o] = fmaxf(0.0f, partial + b1[o]); } } __syncthreads(); // FC2 for (int o = 0; o < 84; ++o) { float partial = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { int idx = local_tid + i * 16; if (idx < 120) { partial += s_fc1[sample][idx] * w2[o * 120 + idx]; } } int base_lane = (tid / 16) * 16; unsigned int mask = 0xFFFF << base_lane; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(mask, partial, offset); } if (local_tid == 0) { s_fc2[sample][o] = fmaxf(0.0f, partial + b2[o]); } } __syncthreads(); // FC3 for (int o = 0; o < 20; ++o) { float partial = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 6; ++i) { int idx = local_tid + i * 16; if (idx < 84) { partial += s_fc2[sample][idx] * w3[o * 84 + idx]; } } int base_lane = (tid / 16) * 16; unsigned int mask = 0xFFFF << base_lane; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(mask, partial, offset); } if (local_tid == 0 && s < batch_size) { output[s * num_classes + o] = partial + b3[o]; } } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with the shuffle mask. `0xFFFF << base_lane` where base_lane = 0 or 16. This gives 0xFFFF or 0xFFFF0000. That's correct for a warp. But `__shfl_down_sync` expects the mask to cover participating threads. For lanes 16..31, mask = 0xFFFF0000. `__shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset)` for lane 16 reads from lane 16+offset within the mask. This works. However, there's a subtlety: `__shfl_down_sync` with a mask only works if all threads in the mask are executing the instruction and not diverged. In our case, all 256 threads in the block are in the same loop, so they should all reach the shuffle. Good. One more issue: for sample=15 (block 15 in a block), the threads are 240..255. These are in warp 7 (lanes 16..31 of warp 7? No: 240/32=7.5, so threads 240..255 are in warp 7, lanes 16..31). So base_lane = (240/16)*16 = 240? No, 240/16 = 15, base_lane = 15*16 = 240. But lane = 240 % 32 = 16. mask = 0xFFFF << 240? That overflows! Oh no. `0xFFFF << base_lane` where base_lane can be 240. That's 32-bit shift by 240, which is undefined for values >= 32. This is wrong! We need the mask relative to the warp, not the block. Correct approach: ``` int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; unsigned int mask = (lane < 16) ? (0xFFFFu) : (0xFFFF0000u); ``` Wait, in warp 0: threads 0..15 have lane 0..15, mask 0xFFFF. Threads 16..31 have lane 16..31, mask 0xFFFF0000. In warp 1: threads 32..47, lanes 0..15 and 16..31 respectively. Same masks. So: ``` unsigned int mask = (lane < 16) ? 0xFFFFu : 0xFFFF0000u; ``` This is independent of warp_id! All warps have the same half-warp grouping. But wait, this assumes that sample boundaries align with warp halves. Let's check: Sample 0: threads 0..15 -> warp 0, lanes 0..15 -> mask 0xFFFF. Good. Sample 1: threads 16..31 -> warp 0, lanes 16..31 -> mask 0xFFFF0000. Good. Sample 2: threads 32..47 -> warp 1, lanes 0..15 -> mask 0xFFFF. Good. So yes, because our grouping is 16 threads per sample and warp size is 32, two samples fit exactly in one warp, and the lane-based mask works perfectly. After shuffle, we need partial to end up in lane 0 or lane 16 (the first lane of each half-warp). `__shfl_down_sync` with offset=8: lane 0 gets from lane 8, lane 1 gets from lane 9, etc. After offset=4, offset=2, offset=1, lane 0 has the sum of lanes 0..15. Similarly, lane 16 has the sum of lanes 16..31. Then we can have any lane write. `if (local_tid == 0)` works because local_tid == 0 means lane is either 0 or 16 (or 32, 48, etc. - actually local_tid = tid % 16. So tid = 0 -> lane 0. tid = 16 -> lane 16. tid = 32 -> lane 0. etc.). So local_tid==0 maps to the first lane of each half-warp, which is exactly where the reduced sum ends up. Perfect. But there's a performance consideration: in the FC1 loop, 120 iterations, each with __syncthreads? No, we don't need __syncthreads inside the loop. The shuffle is within the warp, no cross-warp communication needed. We only need __syncthreads between layers so that all threads finish writing to shared memory before others read it. For FC3, we write directly to global memory. So: `unsigned int mask = (lane < 16) ? 0x0000FFFFu : 0xFFFF0000u;` In CUDA, bitmask for warp shuffle uses unsigned int. Now, what about the case where batch_size is not a multiple of 16? Since they don't write to output (we guard the FC3 write), and they don't read bad memory (we guard the input load), they just waste some compute. That's fine. Load 400 inputs from global to shared memory (s_in). 128 threads each load ~3-4 floats. Coalesced. 2. CUDA doesn't support variable-length arrays in registers for arbitrary indexing with thread indices. But here, each thread only accesses a few elements. But the real issue: in CUDA, `float acc[120];` with dynamic indexing `acc[o]` where o depends on threadIdx.x might spill to local memory if the compiler can't determine the index at compile time. The compiler might allocate registers for all 120 if they're float variables with constant indices, but with variable `o`, it might use indexing. Actually, in CUDA, arrays with indices based on threadIdx.x in a loop often get stored in local memory (spilled) instead of registers. This is because each thread needs its own set, and the compiler can't distribute them across registers if accessed dynamically. To avoid spilling, we should not use arrays but rather keep accumulators scalar and have each thread process one output at a time. Alternative: each thread processes one output (or a few outputs) at a time, keeping the accumulator in a scalar register. Bank number = ((i * 120) + tid) % 32. For fixed i and tid=0: bank = (0 + 0) % 32 = 0. tid=1: bank = (120 + 1) % 32 = 121 % 32 = 25. tid=2: bank = (240 + 2) % 32 = 242 % 32 = 2. tid=3: bank = 363 % 32 = 27. ... For a tile of 32 outputs: - Each output has 8 threads computing partial dot products. - Thread assignment: thread t handles input segment t%8 for output t/8. - 32 outputs * 8 threads = 256 threads total. - For output o, threads with `t/8 == o` cooperate. Warp 1 handles outputs 4..7, etc. For output o (0..3 in warp 0), threads with t in [o*8, o*8+7] cooperate. They all read the same weights for their output o, but different input segments. At iteration i over input features, thread t in group o reads input[i*8 + (t - o*8)] and weight[o][i*8 + ...]. Weight read: all threads in group o read the same weight row o, but different columns. The weight accesses are strided by 400 floats? No, weight[o][col] where col differs. Adjacent threads in the same group read adjacent columns. This is coalesced! In the warp, threads 0..7 and 8..15 read different rows. This might cause a small divergence but weight caching handles it. For input: threads 0..7 read input[0..7], threads 8..15 read input[0..7] (same!). All threads in group o read input[i*8 + local]. If i is the same for all groups, then all 32 threads in the warp read from the same 8 input positions. This is broadcast! Very efficient. So in each iteration i (over 400/8=50 chunks): - Broadcast load 8 inputs from shared memory to all threads. - Each group loads 8 weights from global memory (coalesced within each group, but 4 groups in warp so 4 separate 8-float loads). Still efficient. - 8 FMAs per thread. - 50 iterations. - Total FMAs per thread: 400. After 50 iterations, each thread has a partial sum. Then reduce within group of 8 using shuffle: ``` partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFF, partial, 4); partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFF, partial, 2); partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFF, partial, 1); ``` Now lane 0 of each group has the total sum. Broadcast to all threads in group: ``` partial = __shfl_sync(0xFF, partial, 0); // wait, for a group of 8 within a 32-thread warp ``` Actually, for group size 8 in a warp of 32, the mask for each group is: group 0: threads 0..7, mask = 0xFF group 1: threads 8..15, mask = 0xFF00 group 2: threads 16..23, mask = 0xFF0000 group 3: threads 24..31, mask = 0xFF000000 `__shfl_down_sync` with offset reduces from higher lane to lower lane within the mask. After reduction, lane 0 has the sum for group 0, lane 8 for group 1, etc. We can have those lanes write to shared memory or registers. Then we process the next tile of 32 outputs. For the last chunk of FC3, we need to check `idx < 84` before reading weight. The weight matrix has shape (num_classes, 84). So `w3[o*84 + idx]` is only valid for idx < 84. If idx >= 84, reading is out of bounds (although it might not crash, it's UB). So in the inner loop: ``` int idx = chunk*8 + lane; float inp = 0.0f; float wgt = 0.0f; if (idx < in_features) { inp = s_fc2[idx]; wgt = w3[o * in_features + idx]; } sum += inp * wgt; ``` For FC1: 400 is divisible by 8. No boundary issue. For FC2: 120 is divisible by 8. No boundary issue. For FC3: 84 = 10*8 + 4. For chunk=10, idx ranges 80..87. Only 80..83 are valid. Threads with lane >= 4 read out of bounds. Need conditional. Also for loads: we need to make sure weights are also accessed conditionally. This kernel uses ~2.6KB shared memory. 8 blocks per SM. 100% thread occupancy. Very nice. And each thread does about 400+120+84=604 MACs per sample. For 4096 samples, total 2.5G MACs. At 67 TFLOPS, ~37 microseconds. But with full occupancy and good memory access, it should be efficient. Wait, 604 MACs per thread * 256 threads per block * 4096 blocks = 633M MACs? Let me recalculate: FC1: 120*400 = 48K per sample. FC2: 84*120 = 10,080. FC3: 20*84 = 1,680. Total per sample = 59,760 MACs. Total all samples: 59,760 * 4096 = 244.7M MACs. Yes. At 67 TFLOPS = 67,000 GFLOPS: 244.7 / 67,000 = 0.00365 ms = 3.65 microseconds. With memory overhead and 4 waves, maybe 10-20 microseconds. Still very fast. This cooperative approach is the best of both worlds: efficient memory access, high occupancy, no large shared memory, and simple control flow. Let me write this kernel. Group handling: - tid 0..255 - group = tid / 8 = 0..31 - lane = tid % 8 = 0..7 Warp analysis: Warp 0: threads 0..31. groups 0..3, lanes 0..7 each. Groups in warp 0: group=0 (threads 0..7), group=1 (8..15), group=2 (16..23), group=3 (24..31). All 4 groups use different weights (different output channels o). This means the warp reads 4 different weight rows at each step. But each read within a group is coalesced (8 floats). And the 4 groups read adjacent rows if the outputs are adjacent. For chunk iteration i: - Group 0 reads weight[o0][i*8 .. i*8+7] - Group 1 reads weight[o0+1][i*8 .. i*8+7] - Group 2 reads weight[o0+2][i*8 .. i*8+7] - Group 3 reads weight[o0+3][i*8 .. i*8+7] These are 4 separate 8-float loads. Each is coalesced. They might hit the same cache line or adjacent cache lines depending on weight layout (row-major with 400 columns). Row o is at offset o*400. So group 0 reads from o0*400 + base, group 1 from (o0+1)*400 + base. Distance = 400 floats = 1600 bytes. 1600/128 = 12.5 cache lines apart. So each group reads a different cache line. In a warp, 4 cache lines per chunk iteration. For FC1: 50 chunks * 4 cache lines * 4 groups per warp = 800 cache lines per full tile (32 outputs) per warp. With 8 warps per block, 6400 cache lines per block. 4096 blocks = 26M cache lines. At 128 bytes each, 3.3GB total weight traffic. Since weights are 192KB, and L2 caches them, each block's weight reads come from L2. L2 bandwidth is high, so this is okay. Actually, 26M cache lines sounds like a lot, but they are highly redundant: the same weights are read by all blocks. L2 handles it. The practical bandwidth is much lower than 3.3GB because of caching. Anyway, this design seems solid. Let me write the code. County: for mask in __shfl_down_sync: Each group of 8 threads needs a mask. Since groups are at fixed positions within the warp, the mask for group g in a warp is `0xFF << (g*8)`. But g here is 0..3 within a warp. For thread tid: ``` int lane = tid % 32; int group_in_warp = lane / 8; unsigned int mask = 0xFFu << (group_in_warp * 8); ``` This works because each group of 8 lanes is contiguous. Then: ``` #pragma unroll for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset); } ``` After reduction, lane 0 of each group (lane % 8 == 0) has the total. For writing: `if (lane == 0) s_fc1[o] = ...;` This is clean. Now let me write the full kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_fc_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ w1, const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2, const float* __restrict__ b2, const float* __restrict__ w3, const float* __restrict__ b3, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int num_classes ) { int sample = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (sample >= batch_size) return; __shared__ float s_in[400]; __shared__ float s_fc1[128]; __shared__ float s_fc2[128]; // Load input for (int i = tid; i < 400; i += 256) { s_in[i] = input[sample * 400 + i]; } __syncthreads(); int group = tid / 8; int lane = tid % 8; int lane_in_warp = tid % 32; unsigned int mask = 0xFFu << ((lane_in_warp / 8) * 8); // FC1: 400 -> 120 for (int tile = 0; tile < 4; ++tile) { int out_base = tile * 32; int num_out = (tile == 3) ? 24 : 32; if (group < num_out) { int o = out_base + group; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int chunk = 0; chunk < 50; ++chunk) { int idx = chunk * 8 + lane; float inp = s_in[idx]; float wgt = w1[o * 400 + idx]; sum += inp * wgt; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset); } if (lane == 0) { s_fc1[o] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum + b1[o]); } } } __syncthreads(); // FC2: 120 -> 84 for (int tile = 0; tile < 3; ++tile) { int out_base = tile * 32; int num_out = (tile == 2) ? 20 : 32; if (group < num_out) { int o = out_base + group; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int chunk = 0; chunk < 15; ++chunk) { int idx = chunk * 8 + lane; float inp = s_fc1[idx]; float wgt = w2[o * 120 + idx]; sum += inp * wgt; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset); } if (lane == 0) { s_fc2[o] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum + b2[o]); } } } __syncthreads(); // FC3: 84 -> num_classes int num_tiles = (num_classes + 31) / 32; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) { int out_base = tile * 32; int num_out = min(32, num_classes - out_base); if (group < num_out) { int o = out_base + group; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int chunk = 0; chunk < 11; ++chunk) { int idx = chunk * 8 + lane; float inp = 0.0f; float wgt = 0.0f; if (idx < 84) { inp = s_fc2[idx]; wgt = w3[o * 84 + idx]; } sum += inp * wgt; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset); } if (lane == 0) { output[sample * num_classes + o] = sum + b3[o]; } } } } ``` Wait, for FC1 `num_out`: 120 outputs. 120/32 = 3.75. So tiles 0,1,2 have 32 each (96 outputs). Tile 3 has 24 outputs. `for (int tile = 0; tile < 4; ++tile)` is correct. `int num_out = (tile == 3) ? 24 : 32;` is correct. For FC2: 84 outputs. Tiles 0,1 have 32. Tile 2 has 20. `for (int tile = 0; tile < 3; ++tile)` is correct. For FC3: `num_tiles = (num_classes + 31) / 32`. If num_classes=20, num_tiles=1. Tile 0 has 20 outputs. Correct. One issue: in FC1 and FC2, the `#pragma unroll` on chunk loops with 50 and 15 iterations might increase code size but should be fine. Another issue: `__shfl_down_sync` with mask requires CUDA >= 9.0. H100 is 9.0. Fine. Also, `fmaxf` requires `<cuda_runtime.h>` or `<math.h>`. It should be available. For `s_fc1[idx]` in FC2 chunk loop: idx = chunk*8 + lane. For chunk=14, idx = 112 + lane = 112..119. All valid since s_fc1 has 128 elements and we stored 120. For FC3, `s_fc2[idx]`: chunk=10, idx=80..87. We need to guard idx < 84. I did that with the conditional load. Wait, in FC3, the weight `w3[o*84 + idx]` should also only be loaded if idx < 84. Good, I did that. But what about the `if (idx < 84)` branch? Inside the loop, threads in a warp diverge if some have idx >= 84. For chunk=10, lanes 0..3 have idx 80..83 (valid), lanes 4..7 have idx 84..87 (invalid). So 4 threads execute the if-body, 4 don't. The warp executes both paths. Fine. Now I need to consider if `output[sample * num_classes + o]` is coalesced. In a warp: - Group 0 writes output[sample*nc + out_base + 0] - Group 1 writes output[sample*nc + out_base + 1] - etc. Only one thread per group writes (lane == 0). In a warp of 32 threads, 4 threads write (one per group). These writes are to adjacent memory locations (o, o+1, o+2, o+3). Coalesced! Great. What about the `s_fc1[o]` writes in FC1? Same pattern: lane 0 of each group writes. In a warp, 4 writes per tile. Coalesced. One more thing: between tiles in the same layer, do we need `__syncthreads`? For FC1, threads from different groups write to different locations in `s_fc1`. There's no race. And threads don't read `s_fc1` until after all FC1 tiles are done. Similarly for FC2, writes to `s_fc2` don't conflict. However, within a tile, all threads in a group reduce and then one writes. The next tile starts after all threads in the block finish the previous tile. Since all threads in the block participate in the same `for (tile ...)` loop, and the loop is not divergent across the block (all threads execute the same number of tile iterations, just some groups are masked), the implicit warp synchronization handles within-warp. But across warps, we need `__syncthreads` to ensure all writes to shared memory are visible. Wait, do writes from different warps to `s_fc1` need a barrier? In FC1, wave 0 (threads 0..31) writes to s_fc1[out_base + 0..3] in tile 0. Wave 1 (threads 32..63) writes to s_fc1[out_base + 4..7] in tile 0. If wave 0 finishes tile 0 before wave 1, and starts tile 1, wave 0 might read s_fc1[...] in FC2 before wave 1 finishes writing tile 0 of FC1. But we have `__syncthreads()` between FC1 and FC2. And within FC1, a thread might finish tile 3 and then hit the barrier. But threads in the same block ALL finish the FC1 loop before hitting the `__syncthreads()`. So no problem. Actually, I need to make sure that within the FC1 loop, threads from different warps don't have data hazards. They all write to distinct locations in s_fc1, and they don't read s_fc1 during FC1. So no hazard. We don't need a syncthreads inside the tile loop. Similarly for FC2 and FC3. For FC3, output is written directly to global memory. Different blocks write to different samples. No conflicts. This kernel is very clean. I like it. Total shared memory: 400 + 128 + 128 = 656 floats = 2624 bytes. Very small. Register usage: `sum` scalar + loop vars + a few pointers. Maybe 20-30 registers. Very low. Block size: 256 threads. Occupancy: max possible. This is the winner. Now let me write the C++ wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor fc_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2, torch::Tensor w3, torch::Tensor b3, int num_classes) { int batch_size = input.size(0); auto output = torch::empty({batch_size, num_classes}, input.options()); fused_fc_kernel<<<batch_size, 256>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), w1.data_ptr<float>(), b1.data_ptr<float>(), w2.data_ptr<float>(), b2.data_ptr<float>(), w3.data_ptr<float>(), b3.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_classes ); return output; } ``` One note: `input` is expected to be shape (batch_size, 400). reasoning
The correctness issue likely comes from shared memory initialization or the fact that threads within one warp that don't participate in the reduction may diverge differently. Looking again: - In the `fused_fc_kernel`, I have *four* warps (256 threads), each handling 32 outputs at a time. But I only need 120 outputs. - The `group = tid / 8`, so 32 groups max per tile. - The `lane = tid % 8`. - The mask is `0xFFu << ((lane_in_warp / 8) * 8)`. Each warp has four 8-thread groups. `lane_in_warp / 8` gives 0, 1, 2, or 3. - So mask for the first group in a warp is `0x000000FF`, second is `0x0000FF00`, third is `0x00FF0000`, fourth is `0xFF000000`. - `__shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset)` only shuffles within the masked threads. - But I need to sum *across* all 8 threads in a group. Since threads in a group are `tid % 8` = same lane, they are in the same position within each of the four sub-groups of the warp. - Wait, threads 0-7 have `lane_in_warp / 8 = 0` (mask 0xFF), threads 8-15 have mask 0xFF00, etc. - Thread 0 and thread 8 have `lane = 0`, but different masks! So `__shfl_down_sync` with mask 0xFF for thread 0 will only communicate with threads 0-7. That's correct for group 0 (output 0 within the tile). - Thread 8 has mask 0xFF00, so it communicates with threads 8-15. That's correct for group 1. - So the shuffle reduction is correct for each group. - BUT: what about threads that don't have `group < num_out`? If a thread is not active, does it still execute the shuffle? In the code, the shuffle is inside the `if (group < num_out)` block. If a warp is partially active, the threads outside the `if` don't execute `__shfl_down_sync`. This is fine because they are not in the mask. - Actually, the issue is that `s_fc1` and `s_fc2` are declared with sizes 120 and 84. The shared memory is fine. Wait, look at `conv1_relu_maxpool_kernel`. I output to `[batch * 1176 + c * 196 + ph * 14 + pw]`. This is for a tensor of shape `[batch_size, 6, 14, 14]`. `c * 196` is correct. `ph * 14 + pw` is correct. This matches PyTorch's NCHW format. Wait, but PyTorch `F.max_pool2d` default behavior: `return_indices=False`. The output shape after conv1 is `[batch, 6, 28, 28]`. Max pool with kernel=2, stride=2 gives `[batch, 6, 14, 14]`. Yes. For conv2, input is `[batch, 6, 14, 14]`. Conv2 with kernel=5, stride=1 gives `[batch, 16, 10, 10]`. Max pool gives `[batch, 16, 5, 5]`. Yes. Are my convolutions correct? Conv1: `F.relu(self.conv1(x))`. `self.conv1` is `nn.Conv2d(1, 6, 5, stride=1)`. No padding. Input: `[batch, 1, 32, 32]`. Output size = `(32 - 5) + 1 = 28`. Correct. My kernel loops `h` from `ph*2` to `ph*2+1`, `w` from `pw*2` to `pw*2+1`. For each `(h, w)` in the *pre-pool* conv output: `sum = b` For `kh` in 0..4, `kw` in 0..4: `inp = input[batch, 0, h+kh, w+kw]` `wgt = weight[c, 0, kh, kw]` This is correct. Then `val = relu(sum)`. Then `max_val = max(max_val, val)`. Then `output[batch, c, ph, pw] = max_val`. This is correct. Wait! Are there padding issues? `nn.Conv2d` default padding is 0. So no padding. My indices `h+kh` and `w+kw` range from 0 to 31. Since `h` max is `4*2+1 = 9`, `h+kh` max is `9+4 = 13`. Wait, that's for `ph=4`? `ph` ranges from 0 to 4 (since `tid < 25`, `ph = tid/5` ranges 0..4). For `ph=4`, `h = 8` or `9`. `h+kh` max = `9+4 = 13`. But the conv1 output is 28x28! The pre-pooling h/w should range from 0 to 27. If `ph` goes 0..4 and `pw` goes 0..4, then max pool output is 5x5, not 14x14! Wait, I made a mistake. Conv1 output: 28x28. Max pool with kernel=2, stride=2: output is 14x14. So `ph` should range 0..13, `pw` 0..13. That's 196 elements. But my kernel uses `tid < 25`, so `ph` 0..4, `pw` 0..4. That's only 25 elements, corresponding to a 5x5 output! Oh my god. I confused the output sizes. Conv1 output after pool is 14x14 = 196. I need 196 threads per (batch, channel), not 25. Conv2 output after pool is 5x5 = 25. That one is correct. That's the bug! And adjust the indexing: `int ph = tid / 14;` `int pw = tid % 14;` `h = ph * 2 + ph_off` (0..27) `w = pw * 2 + pw_off` (0..27) This is correct for 14x14 output. i < 400; i += blockDim.x) {\n s_in[i] = input[sample * 400 + i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n int group = tid / 8;\n int lane = tid % 8;\n int lane_in_warp = tid % 32;\n unsigned int mask = 0xFFu << ((lane_in_warp / 8) * 8);\n\n for (int tile = 0; tile < 4; ++tile) {\n int out_base = tile * 32;\n int num_out = (tile == 3) ? 24 : 32;\n if (group < num_out) {\n int o = out_base + group;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int chunk = 0; chunk < 50; ++chunk) {\n int idx = chunk * 8 + lane;\n float inp = s_in[idx];\n float wgt = w1[o * 400 + idx];\n sum += inp * wgt;\n }\n for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n sum += __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset);\n }\n if (lane == 0) {\n s_fc1[o] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum + b1[o]);\n }\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n for (int tile = 0; tile < 3; ++tile) {\n int out_base = tile * 32;\n int num_out = (tile == 2) ? 20 : 32;\n if (group < num_out) {\n int o = out_base + group;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int chunk = 0; chunk < 15; ++chunk) {\n int idx = chunk * 8 + lane;\n float inp = s_fc1[idx];\n float wgt = w2[o * 120 + idx];\n sum += inp * wgt;\n }\n for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n sum += __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset);\n }\n if (lane == 0) {\n s_fc2[o] = fmaxf(0.0f, sum + b2[o]);\n }\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n int num_tiles = (num_classes + 31) / 32;\n for (int tile = 0; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n sum += __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset);\n }\n if (lane == 0) {\n output[sample * num_classes + o] = sum + b3[o];\n }\n }\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fc_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1,\n torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2,\n torch::Tensor w3, torch::Tensor b3, int num_classes) {\n int batch_size = input.size(0);\n auto output = torch::empty({batch_size, num_classes}, input.options());\n fused_fc_kernel<<<batch_size, 256>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n w1.data_ptr<float>(), b1.data_ptr<float>(),\n w2.data_ptr<float>(), b2.data_ptr<float>(),\n w3.data_ptr<float>(), b3.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, num_classes);\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = r'''\ntorch::Tensor fc_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1,\n torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2,\n torch::Tensor w3, torch::Tensor b3, int num_classes);\n'''\n\next = load_inline(\n name='lenet_fused_fc_v1',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['fc_forward_cuda'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, num_classes):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.num_classes = num_classes\n self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels=1, out_channels=6, kernel_size=5, stride=1)\n self.conv2 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels=6, out_channels=16, kernel_size=5, stride=1)\n self.fc1 = nn.Linear(in_features=16*5*5, out_features=120)\n self.fc2 = nn.Linear(in_features=120, out_features=84)\n self.fc3 = nn.Linear(in_features=84, out_features=num_classes)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n x = F.relu(self.conv1(x))\n x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=2, stride=2)\n x = F.relu(self.conv2(x))\n x = F.max_pool2d(x, kernel_size=2, stride=2)\n x = x.view(-1, 16*5*5)\n x = ext.fc_forward_cuda(\n x,\n self.fc1.weight, self.fc1.bias,\n self.fc2.weight, self.fc2.bias,\n self.fc3.weight, self.fc3.bias,\n self.num_classes\n )\n return x\n" }
